Not Recommended
14.0 hrs on record
Posted: 11 September, 2020
Takes about 15 hours to do a 100% play-through, or closer to 10 hours if you just want the core story.


Positives
• The first chapter (of the game's three) has an interesting story with twists that keep you interested in what will happen next.
• Unique methods of story-telling that aren't typically used for this type of game.
• A large amount of background story to find for people who take the time to explore the areas and read through everything.
• They finished the entire game before releasing it. Episodes are released a week apart rather than months apart.


Negatives
• As with all of Dontnod's games, the ending isn't satisfying and the final choice makes no sense in relation to the game's story.
• So many choices have very short time restrictions for no reason before the game just makes a choice for you. The timer bar is very small and hard to notice.
• Very easy to miss important things because you say or do something not knowing it will force the story to progress and lock you out of an area or interaction.
• Characters always react to long pieces of text immediately when they're opened, before you have a chance to read them.
• Characters sometimes interupt themselves or each other for less important or less interesting dialogue because they are scripted to walk around and talk about certain things after set amounts of time.
• Characters allow very little time to explore an area before they start their constant pestering about moving on to whatever you're intended to do to progress the story.
• It can often be difficult to find the exact place the game wants you to stand or point the camera to interact with something.
• Even though the episodes are not sold individually and were released only a week apart, the first two episodes still have post-credits spoiler videos for the following episode.

Not Recommended
12.1 hrs on record
Posted: 7 March, 2021
I loved all the Life is Strange games but this one did not cut it. Boring is the best way I can describe it. Its a game that I only finished out of a sense of obligation. I was actually looking forward to it and bought it almost as soon as it came out. I only just finished it.




*** LOTS OF SPOILERS BELOW ***

The scenery is really nice, especially in the first episode where you're first introduced to the setting. That's really about the only nice thing I have to say about the game.

The abilities of the main characters to manifest their memories in the real world was neat but it doesn't really come up except where the story needs it. At that point its like, why not just have them remember things normally? The scenes of memories shown could have just been flashbacks. The magical element didn't add anything.
They should have left it more open; like you can discover memories on your own instead of having to do it at certain points to move the story forward. Like in Life is Strange where you can use the rewind power to alter all kinds of things in the world around you that aren't even related to the main story.
And the psychic stuff is never questioned or explained. I figured that would be a big part of the conclusion and would be related to who their father was but nope. They just have powers, happens all the time. Admittedly, LIS didn't explain the origin of the powers but they didn't gloss over it like its no big deal; it was a major focus. Same with LIS2 where it was pretty much the MAIN focus of the game.

The puzzles were tedious and elicited a loud groan from me every time they came up. They all involve having to read through a 30-page children's book. And they weren't even integrated in the events of the story, they were just literal puzzles. They didn't feel organic or compelling; they felt like padding. The one exception was where you had to dig through the police archive records.
And the final puzzle that the mother left behind felt so unnatural. She spent all that time and effort creating a room full of puzzles to reveal deeply personal things. If it was meant to hide these secrets, why make it at all? If it was meant to be found by Tyler and Alyson, then why make it a game? Just leave a letter explaining what you want them to know. And if it was so secret, how did Tom know about it?

The characters are not interesting and mostly seem just whiny and unsympathetic. Tyler is just unbearable. Alyson is unremarkable and hollow. There weren't really any big stakes for them. No matter how the story ended, they would be fine so I couldn't really care about their investigation. It would have been more interesting to set the game when they were kids with a mother that is losing touch with reality. Or maybe flash back and forth between the two time periods. The whole game was like a prologue to a more interesting story.
The end was disappointing and so inconsequential that the game itself was like "just pick an ending, it doesn't matter". It literally had you choose the reality that you wanted to be true rather than actually getting to the bottom of the mystery. Then what the hell was the point? We could have done that on day one.
And regardless of the reality you chose, nothing changed. The kids would have ended up mostly the same way, either adopted or sent to some orphanage or similar facility. It was a waste of my and their time to investigate it.

Life is Strange was a masterpiece. Not perfect but whatever faults it had were far outweighed by its merits. This game is the opposite; its merits were far outweighed by its faults.

Not Recommended
13.1 hrs on record
Posted: 10 October, 2021
They had me in the first half, not gonna lie.

I am no stranger to interactive movies masquerading as games, like Telltale's The Walking Dead. These games bank on their stories, characters, and world to engross the player and deliver an enjoyable experience. But we also saw some much-needed gameplay innovation. Detroit: Become Human, Heavy Rain, Until Dawn, all have this everpresent uncertainty of whether a single "wrong" choice or a tardily button press would spell doom for your protagonist.

So, does Tell Me Why bring something new to the table? No, it does not. It's a pretty generic story-driven game with some okayish bits. The mystery surrounding the events that unfolded on that fateful night and Mary-Ann's life pushed me to play so I could see how everything would conclude. I also liked how Michael and the edgy lone wolf, Tyler, were written. Both are part of the LGBTQ+ community, but neither has their sexual orientation or gender as their defining character trait nor is Tyler's transition to a transgender man used as the only focal point of the story. It's just another part of the puzzle called "The Life of the Ronans." So, props to the writers for creating compelling and believable characters in these two. The others... Well, they could've used some of that love as well. They weren't bad, but I found them lacking, as they mostly filled a specific stereotype that was simply there to push the story further. None of their actions left me surprised, nor did I care for them. But the only one that really disappointed me was Alyson. I can't put my finger on it, but she was just plain, uninteresting, and I never enjoyed playing as her.


I mean, it says, "This is not a scam!" so it must be true. I'm in! 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Sv. Prolivije

But this is where this mostly fun train ride ends.

Sv. Prolivije did not like that!

Let's start with the interactions between the twins, Alyson and Tyler. It felt fake and forced. I never felt like they genuinely cared for one another or were close. But the game made it its mission to tell me this as often as possible. And if said enough times, I guess that somehow makes it true. The siblings felt more like stage actors who took this gig for a quick buck. It's also absurd how much they fight and how quickly they forgive one another. It's as if it was purposefully written this way to stir up some fake tension and drama between the two.

Now, onto the "amazing" bond system between the siblings. It's either deepened by being nice to everyone or worsened by being an arse, justified or not. And this just did not work for me. Why? Well, some people really did deserve the arse reaction. So, instead of telling someone off, who lied and betrayed your trust, you should be nice to them. You don't want to hurt your sibling's feelings or something.

But what does it matter when your actions won't affect the ending? Sorry, I should rephrase that — only two choices in Chapter 3 will influence the ending. Expanding further would require heavy spoilers. And since some still might want to play and judge the game themselves, I'm gonna abstain from going into further detail. But know, each of the two scenarios in Chapter 3 that determine the ending you get will offer you two choices — one makes sense and fits the story, and the other doesn't. Simple. This pretty much made the "twins bond" stuff irrelevant. But so are all the choices leading up to this chapter. So, feel free to be an arse when the time is right without fearing that you will get a bad ending or something.


Family reunions are always fun 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Sv. Prolivije

Speaking of bad endings, remember Mass Effect 3 and its "awesome" endings (The red ending is finally canon! Woooo!!)? Well, the endings to our duo's journey are basically that — just two differently coloured endings. The final chapter felt like it was on rails, with poor pacing compared to the previous parts. But oh boy, is the story riddled with plot holes and conveniences just so the story can happen. Shakespeare who?

There was one scene where I thought (and hoped) the story would take a more supernatural turn. Maybe the twins, constantly using their "voice", a form of telepathic communication between the two, somehow manifested the menacing Mad Hunter from their stories. And it was he who had a hand in all of this. But nope. Instead, my more boring prediction came true. Now, I'm a little sad my silly supernatural thingy wasn't the correct one.

Anyway, let's wrap this up.

The last part is the gameplay. While I don't judge the gameplay of story-driven games too harshly, Tell Me Why feels outdated. Even Telltale's The Walking Dead: The Final Season had other forms of player interaction besides simply walking around and pressing A. You got to shoot and use traps to kill zombies. Here, you walk, press A to hear pointless dialogue or watch short memories from the twins' past, then press A some more until a cutscene triggers and repeat. On occasion, the game may introduce a puzzle or two. But they were mostly a nuisance. The solutions were simple, yet finding them was tedious. You have to scour the book from our twins' childhood, searching for clues hidden within its stories. Thankfully, you can also pry open any lock — there's always a handy tool somewhere near. So, at least Don't Nod were self-aware enough about the quality of their puzzles to include this skip.


*sad american noises* 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Sv. Prolivije

And lastly, there are no quick-time events, time-limited or button-mashing sections, and during the dialogue that affects the story, you have all the time in the world to decide your answer. However, the story parades this constant sense of urgency for some reason. The twins MUST sell their house and move away from their hometown as soon as possible. Why? So the story can happen, of course! It's just poor writing, done to create an artificial sense of urgency in the player. And it's clear — the gameplay lets you dillydally all you want with zero repercussions.

In the end, I wasted my time playing this game. I liked some of the characters and the mystery surrounding Mary-Ann and her life. But once all the pieces were in place, the payoff was underwhelming and not worth the wait. Pair it with tedious gameplay, used only to prolong the game's runtime, and you have a perfect recipe for a "masterpiece."

Also, how are you gonna call your game Tell Me Why and not include a single kart racing easter egg or collectable? For shame Don't Nod, for shame!

To those who made it this far, you're all majestic sausages. And if you enjoy this review, come and consume more wisdom from the Gospel of Sv. Prolivije.
 

Sv. Prolivije

The positive side of this game is the visuals - the colours and mountains and water made me want to visit Alaska.
BUT I can't really recommend this game because of one main reason - the game didn't really feel like one.

I love walking simulators and story-based games, but especially this kind of games have to offer a user a fair game. I simply didn't feel like I had enough choices. User is usually offered only two choices, and it's pretty clear which one is "correct" in the opinion of developers. Anyway, both choices are usually like that: something aggressive and unresonable and something extremely understanding and unrealistic. The world isn't like that and I love when such games show us all the shades of grey that are between being a total jerk and a saint. I was really disappointed in this aspect of the game as I've read reviews about a masterpiece storytelling etc. For me it felt more like interacting with the "perfect" people for 8 hours. I don't know, maybe I live in an emotionally barren (or filled with flawed people) world or something like that but I don't think people are so forgiving, so understanding and so ready to admit their mistakes as in this game.

TLDR; Not enough choices, felt like I was forced into playing an "ideal human being"

This is a good game, it really shows Dontnod knows how to craft an interesting experience, but they really need better writers. There is nothing inherently wrong with the story, but as often is the case with choice & consequence games, it really shines through how there is a right way and a wrong way to choose, this should not be the case in what is marketed as an open story where you decide the outcome. An example in this game is how the twins remember things, and can look back at their memories, but sometimes their memories are slightly different (Tyler remembers somebody as being angry, and Alyson remembers the same someone as being sad) and everytime so far, picking Alysons memory is "wrong" and sours your relationship with your twin. I don't like this and it feels like forced narrative, the game is basically centered around Tyler and if you don't agree with him, it's the wrong choice according to the game. Same thing when Tyler is angry at a person very close to Alyson, if you defend that person boom it's the wrong choice, like the game wanted you the player to make Alyson throw a person very close to her under the bus for an old grudge Tyler holds.
Well anyway aside from this the game is well made and interesting. I would recommend it but what I talked about is really weighing down the positives. I'm not finished with the story so I might update this.

Update: So I finished the game, the problems I talked about persists through the game. ultimately feeling like I watched the story play out, not affecting what happens really. If steam had a Yellow rating or a sideways thumb I would rate it that. For now I will say buy the game on a sale, the story is worth experiencing, it has that DontNod feel. But the choice & consequence gameplay is not very strong and not worth buying it for that aspect alone.

Not Recommended
9.3 hrs on record
Posted: 12 September, 2020
I really wanted to like this game, but I honestly struggle to find anything positive to say about it. I liked the idea of trying to piece together old memories, and the plot was quite intriguing to begin with. Besides that pretty much the only thing I can praise is the visuals. Whilst obviously not on par with big AAA titles, it's a far improvement on DONTNOD's previous games, from the atmosphere of the environment to the tiny details in each location, it's pretty nice to look at. The characters are okay, though I sometimes find them inconsistent.

Now, onto the bad things...

Janky Controls
The characters are difficult to move around, which often makes it hard to interact with the points of interest in the environment. There's no run button, but your character will start running when the game decides it's necessary. The memory mechanic is fiddly and awkward to use, as are the dialogue options when they appear.

Boring Gameplay
DONTNOD's games are often slow, but this game was absolutely tedious to slog through. The storybook theme wasn't interesting at all, and I had no fun solving any of the puzzles. One of my favourite things to do in these kinds of games is to look around at everything, and interact with it all to hear the characters' thoughts on things. In this game, almost every time you interact with something there is another character around who will comment on whatever your player character has said, which leads every bit of exploring into several lines of dialogue.

(Also, if your character is alone while you're exploring, rather than hearing their thoughts the character is actually speaking out loud to themselves, which was really weird once I noticed it.)

The Storybook Theme
This may be a personal preference but the storybook theme got old really fast. Reading through some kids' fairytales to work out puzzles was SO boring. Listening to two 20-year-olds constantly talking about goblins and referring to characters as their storybook counterparts was really cringey.

Frustrating Pacing/Plot Progression
It takes characters so long to work out some obvious details that I felt like I was always two steps ahead of the plot. There are many instances in the game where the characters would find out crucial information to progress the plot, and then just... not act on it. If I've discovered an interesting story revelation I want to find out more, not screech to a halt to do a tedious fetch quest and a bunch of dialogue I don't care about. On the other hand, there are many times where the game tries to push you towards the plot during times when I actually want to explore.

Your Choices Don't Matter
This is the biggest problem I have with this game. LiS 1 had some great branching consequences and LiS 2 tweaked the mechanic to make it even better, so I had high hopes for this game. Turns out your choices do not matter whatsoever.

There's a little dialogue puzzle in Episode 1 where you try to get some information from a side character. On my first playthrough I didn't pass this, but in the next scene my character's attitude did a total 180 and I was really confused. After I went back and replayed the scene to pass the puzzle, the exact same cutscene happened (with some minor dialogue changes), and I realised it made much more sense that way.

This happens with every choice. It doesn't matter what dialogue options I choose, the twins will either argue or talk it out depending on what the game wants. In Episode 3 there is a major piece of information that you can choose to reveal or keep to yourself; something that should change one of the established relationships completely. Again, some different dialogue happens but the end cutscene of that section is exactly the same no matter what you choose.

What's the point in having "choices" if it makes absolutely no difference at the end of the scene?

Unsatisfying Plot
Even with all of the above problems, I pushed through to the end of Episode 3 because I was interested to see where the plot was heading. Needless to say, I was hugely disappointed. Without spoiling the ending, it was completely unsatisfying.

Overall, a massive disappointment that I just can't recommend. I might pick it up again to grind the achievements but I have no desire to ever replay the story.

Not Recommended
10.5 hrs on record
Posted: 3 October, 2020
WARNING - CONTAINS SPOILERS

I’ve broken this review up into three sections. The Good (stuff I liked), The Bad (stuff I didn’t like), and The Ugly (stuff I really didn’t like). If you’re not in a reading mood, there’s a tl;dr at the bottom.


THE GOOD
(1) There's no two ways about it; Tell Me Why looks gorgeous. The environments are beautifully rendered, and I could probably stare at the water animation all day.The audio to first-rate, too; particularly the background noise. There were a couple of times I’d hear a sound in-game (a plane flying overhead or someone talking) and I’d take my headphones off to check if it had been real.

(2) I liked the way the player alternates between both siblings, allowing them to experience things from different perspectives and demonstrating how the game characters react differently to each twin.

(3) The story has a lot of interesting hooks and maintains an air of mystery that makes the player curious for more.


THE BAD
(1) Although the plot seemed to show promise, it never really felt like it went anywhere. There were scenes and story elements that were likely added to give the world a more complete feel, but many of them end up feeling like weird loose ends. Scenes like this add up to make half the game feel like a string of pointless conversations. For example, 

(2) The controls are kind of awkward. I was using an Xbox-style gamepad to play, and more often than not trying to move the characters around resulted in them jinking around like they were only passingly familiar with how walking was supposed to work.

(3) The game doesn’t have multiple save slots, the automatic save points could be pretty far between, and there’s no manual save function. That means that if you have to turn the game off before reaching one, you might end up losing up to an hour of progress. It also means that if you make a mistake (like accidentally selecting the wrong response in a conversation) you have to quit to the main menu and start over from the last auto-save, with no way to skip over any of the cut-scenes.

(4) While there aren’t too many of them, Tell Me Why does include a few non-optional (and largely pointless) quicktime events. One of them comes up when  It does nothing to advance the story and ends up feeling like a complete waste of time.

(5) There are multiple occasions where the twins recall the details of a past event differently, and the player has to choose which one is ‘true’. Now matter how you feel about it as a player, picking the memory of the twin you’re not controlling at that time gives you a positive result. Alternatively, if you choose the memory of the twin you’re controlling at that time, it upsets the other one and damages your bond as siblings. This make them feel less like legitimate choices and more like true/false questions.

(6) The playthrough time is only about 10 hours. Compared to the 20-25 hours of gameplay in Life is Strange or the 30+ hours in Life is Strange 2, this really isn’t a lot.


THE UGLY
(1) There’s an element to the game that is somehow made a core element of the story and completely glossed over at the same time. 

(2) A lot of the characters reacted to big things in unusually subdued or unusual ways. For example, 

(3) A lot of the dialogue prompts are misleading, leading to a different reaction than you intended to have. For instance, you could select one that says “I don’t think so.” expecting it to sound hesitant, and instead you’ll end up sounding petulant or argumentative, ultimately pissing off whoever you’re talking to.

(4) There are, for all intents and purposes, two endings to the game. In the ‘happy’ ending,  In the other ending,  I admittedly never got to this ending myself, since it requires the player to make a ton of negative choices. I ended up having to watch it on YouTube.

(5) In the end, nothing really comes of the events of the game. Perhaps, the twins understand their past a little better, but there’s no big or life-changing decisions. For instance, 


SUMMARY
Tell Me Why is a visually lovely game with amazing audio and completely gorgeous scenery. Unfortunately, it suffers from mildly awkward controls and a poor auto-save system. Its storyline, while interesting, fails to meet its potential and never really comes together in the end.

Not Recommended
34.5 hrs on record
Posted: 14 February, 2021
Similar to Dontnod's Life Is Strange, Tell Me Why is an interactive feature movie focusing on the story of twins in a small Alaskan town, trying to find out who their mother really was and why she acted as she did in the past.

The story is very slow paced, the characters are uninspiring and the game feels like it will never finally come to an end — Dontnod really made the scenes painfully slow. The best example is probably the scene in the store where we're counting the cans, putting stickers to the furniture and …

Probably the only highlight of Tell Me Why are the fairy tale world created by the twins mum Mary-Ann, in the Book of Goblins, and the accurately and thoughtfully executed representation of trans protagonist Tyler.

The game's intention is to focus on the dialogues, however they are too often pretty meaningless chat instead of well written conversations — why not let characters flirt instead of letting them count the tomato cans? There was a plenty of space for a few serious or funny conversations.

When compared to the Life Is Strange, some people noted the «much improved graphics style», which I see quite the opposite way — the graphic style of Tell Me Why is pretty much failed.

The reason why is simple: In Life Is Strange we get that dreamy tempera + gouache look which is resembling of a hand drawn game — in Tell Me Why, we get improved detail, but in a bad mixture of high detailed characters and low detail surroundings, which kind of don't belong together.

The town is empty, environmental details are hardly available, the lifeless facial expressions of the characters do not reflect emotions. Paired with the cumbersome investigating of the environment and missing music …

On top of it, we also get some really awkward and annoying «chasing the memories» game mechanics, which requires us to find them first (upon the right trigger event), walking around with the space key pressed down until we found them and then focus on them by holding a mouse button.

https://steamcommunity.com/app/1180660/discussions/0/2941370278930188816/#c5051317596404336828

Also, when I already talk about the technical execution, that new graphic style now needs DirectX 12 and uses 4 GB VRAM. I'd very much prefer the old, «moody» graphic style, which used DirectX 9 and 1 GB VRAM.

This new style still looks pretty old, when compared to 7 y/o «The Vanishing of Ethan Carter». The original Life Is Strange looked way more original (== better) and would run even on some old Intel HD 3000 on-chip graphics without dedicated memory (shared 256 MB DDR 3 RAM suffices!).

Hard to recommend.

Posted: 2 April, 2022
I was really hoping to like this game since Life is Strange was one of my favorite games and had one the best stories in any videogame I have ever played. So my expectations were honestly high. However, despite the game looked good and the voices and acting were pretty impressive, the story behind it was dumb, dull and unintesresting. None of the characters are likeable, not even the main characters. There is nothing you can really think of to connect or relate to with them, as opposed to those from LiS for instance. Here they do not have personality, and many are just secondary ones with absolutely no relevance whatsoever in the game. Although it touches upon many controversial issues (suicide, transgender, alcoholism, etc...) many situations feel forced, not making sense or feeling too real, as though they were just fillers. But the most important thing about this game that really got up to my nerves was the pace of the gameplay. It is extremely slow and boring and 90% of the time you basically do nothing but either passing lines and reading or walk around looking for objects or persons to interact with, most of which are completely skippable and/or irrelevant. Sometimes you can even end up getting stuck in a loop conversation that cannot be skipped, or just have to walk at a very slow pace to one place to the other, or end up passing an invisible no-return point and you miss whatever you left behind. At some point, without realizing I was yawning and wishing the game ended asap to move on. I thought, well maybe it gets better and the story has a big turnout in the end or there is a twist-plot or something. But I was wrong, the end is meh and it all comes down to basically choosing what to believe or what you think is more likely to have have happened. The "remember things" ability seemed interesting and could have been something quite useful, similar to what happened in LiS with Max and the ability to go back in time. However, in TmW it only serves to see short flashbacks, nothing more, quite a waste to make something cool out of it honestly...Finally, decisions do not matter, maybe one or two at some points and very minor, but the big majority do not go beyond having slightly different conversations or lines from specific characters without any influence on the story. Overall, it was quite a disappointment, boring game, bad story and forgettable characters.

10 people found this review funny 6

Not Recommended
9.8 hrs on record
Posted: 28 August, 2020
Product received for free

I love games created by Dontnod Entertainment. Life is Strange is probably the 2nd-3rd best story driven cinematic “choices matter” game I ever experienced (First is Telltale’s Walking Dead, I believe the story of Clementine is unbeatable in this category). While Vampyr had some bad combat, it was also saved by the very good story. I believe so, that Tell Me Why doesn't have the quality we usually get from this developer team.

Right now it feels that the review score has been brought by the fear of downvoting a game with its current theme. While Tell me Why has a message, I believe it is important for a story to be well written and interesting, which pulls in the player at the first second and not letting us away until the end.

The game has a very promising start. We learn about a family tragedy which causes the protagonist twin siblings to get apart while they are still very young. The gameplay start as the now adult twins reunite, so they can face their past and move on. Episode 1 is usually not the most emotional chapter in these kinds of games but Dontnod managed to make it extremely boring.

We learn about the superpower of the twins, that they can see the past and they have telepathically bonded with each other. The premise of the game is great. While one of the twins is a trangender human being and there are references in the story that he had a tough childhood because of that the writers failed to deliver any emotional memories to the players about this. In the 3 hours of the Episode we see just happy memories, that they both were loved by their mom, and while their mom was clearly fighting with inner demons there was a question in the air all the time: What was the problem which caused the tragedy? Based on the memories the game showed us, the tragic events were not exactly expected. During this “prologue” it was easy to abandon the game, I felt like it is a work to keep playing and the biggest problem of it? It is 33% of the total game, not just in the number of Episodes, but also in the total playtime.

Then we get to the ending of the first part of the game. The cliffhanger there is the first heartbreaking and emotional scene. It gave at least some hope for Episode 2, which was now actually better. The twins try to figure more about their past, they try to get better knowledge on their mother and about the inner shadows that haunted her. It started to be clear that only one of the twins is a notable character. Alyson became much more interesting and emotional. It could be because of the significantly better voice acting on her. Tyler is a different case, it felt so that the writers had no clue what to do with a transgender character and the forced LGBTQ romance with a side character felt more distracting and slowed down the story which was already bleeding. I blame the writers on that. The character of Tyler had a huge potential.

Then Episode 3 arrived and it was now finally on the standard of Dontnod. Alyson's character literally made the episode to a one protagonist show. His fight with the past and keeping mental integrity while getting released of it's burden is the emotional high point of the story.

The twins reveal the secrets and history of their mom at the end of the game through some very nice and interesting puzzles. We will get our happy or bad ending then at the end, based on the decisions we had during the gameplay, just like another important story at the end based on our single decision there.


Conclusion

It is worth playing if you have XBOX game pass because of some other game but purchasing it directly for the asked price? Nope, the actual valuable content of the game is way too short filled with empty stories.

Story: 5/10 Expected more from Dontnod.
Replay value: 1/10 Story driven game and the different decisions not influencing the story radically.
Atmosphere: 6/10 Game is easy to stop during episode 1 but then it gets some “moments’.
Voice Acting: 6/10 Tyler feels bad, Alyson exceptional and some characters have an extremely neutral and boring voice acting.
Visuals: 9/10 Nice looking game.
Music/Sound effects: 9/10.
Game difficulty: 3/10 There are some puzzles in the game, but no gameplay challenges in the game, no quick time events either.

Hey, I am an achievement hunter!
Perfection time: ~14-18h. Nearly two whole playthroughs required as 2 achievements awarded for the two endings and that is determined by the decisions you made through the whole game. It might be an option to focus on one in episode 1, then focus on another one on episode 2+3 so need to replay only from the start of episode 2. I can’t confirm this yet, not sure the different decisions outweigh E1 decisions. There are also collectibles and many missable achievements, but there are many guides already for the game.

Disclaimer: Game was given to me for review purposes by Microsoft/Xbox studios.

If you liked the review or you like to earn achievements, but in enjoyable, fun and entertaining games, then please follow my Average Achievement Hunter curator or just check out our curated games. Thanks

I have played through the first chapter of Tell Me Why, I am on the fence and wish I could give it a 'middling rating'. I love story based games, but what I have seen so far, Tyler is such an unlikable character; he is controlling over his sister Alyson in the story, flashbacks show his selfish side. I know in the game the trauma they have both faced, but at least make your main character likeable. For example, I do really like Alyson in contrast, she sympathises with her brother and is the most logical and well mannered of the two, Tyler is resentful all the time (I know, trauma), but in the opening act it just makes players not like the character. I will say though, the setting is beautiful and it is very believable, and I will play it to the end, as I respect the awareness it raises. I just wish Tyler could have been written better, Disco Elysium is an example of superbly written multi-layered characters.

Edit one: I have now played through Episode Two and it is much better than Episode One, my original points still stand for first impressions. Tyler can still react negatively to other people, but you now have the ability at many points to change the outcomes of certain situations, which can make him become more likeable. Episode One really needed a stronger start, for example; Dontnod could have gave the player more agency in how to react in Episode One, which is present in Episode Two. They feel completely different in terms of quality of writing too.

Sumol
4
14 people found this review helpful 0

Not Recommended
11.6 hrs on record
Posted: 27 February, 2021
Chekhov's gun. Mary-Ann’s shotgun

Don't forget to visit my Curator's page for more recommendations!


Chekhov's gun (Russian: Чеховское ружьё) is a dramatic principle that states that every element in a story must be necessary, and irrelevant elements should be removed.

What do we have here?

Here we have a thoughtfully written story (but which is more suitable for a film rather than an interactive game) which raises questions about LGBT community, family issues and other “hard” questions which people usually discuss with a therapist.
The story is about two twins which after dramatical events were separated for 10 years and now finally they are meeting up to sell the house where they spent their childhood.
The game consists of three chapters, which are really distinguished in manner of narrative from each other which unfortunately doesn’t serve the game well. The twins have a special ability which allows them to recapture their memories and to communicate telepathically for cheating on exams, to help them to understand what really has happened that night and years before their separation. Following the old memories we observe how Tyler and Allyson differently perceive their memories and all the skeletons in the closet which they find.


Chapter 2 intro 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Fontrevi


Some technical stuff

The visuals are looking fantastic: the snow-covered mountains, frozen lakes, and lights yes-yes 10 years the lights in the house were still on. I guess the bill would be quite high in the house were what I enjoyed it the most.
Gameplay is a bit boring, and the characters are moving quite slow. The memories recapture didn’t work correctly for me all the time. There were few locations where firstly I had to spin around a few times and then I was able to find a point where I could “activate” memories. Also I experienced some issues in stacking in textures, especially in the wooden house so I had to restart the game.

Magnificent mountains view 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Fontrevi


Some gameplay stuff

I was really excited that it is the same developer as Life is Strange (there is even an easter egg related to LiS) but Tell Me Why falls short not only of Life is Strange and also Life is Strange: Before the Storm.
I think the game had much bigger potential if there was bigger empathy of the main characters but as for me the supportive characters are looking better written.
While playing the game I was disappointed with the decision-making. In narrative games such as this one, being able to choose the option most suitable for you is the main reason for playing such a game. However, when the main feature isn’t presented as intended, the game loses its genre and removes the real reason for playing it.
Many times it wasn’t me who drove the story, but the story drove itself. I wanted to act differently, but the game couldn’t provide enough options or at least a more satisfying explanation of motives. Choice-selection wise the game is really poor and there are mostly black and white choices, which could be seen in the statistics at the end of the chapters (most of the decisions were on 80/20 ratio). In comparison, just remember how the choices made by players were balanced in Life is Strange where there were only “grey” options which are neither good or bad. And this, I believe, is the main limitation of the game and I told you why the game didn’t achieve that success as Life is Strange.
There are really two or three choices which really matter for the end of the story, which makes most of the dialogue meaningless and provides the questions but not the answers. It is like with (yes I’m gonna tell the same joke twice) Chekhov’s gun Mary-Ann’s shotgun – in the game many interesting moments but they fade away as they do not provide any continuation. Speaking about the real gun, 


That also affects the words from the annotation “their past is not what they remember” which boosted my general interest in the game. However, in the end of the game I was slightly disappointed that I got caught as I expected something else from the game.


The indoor lights 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: Fontrevi


A quick note for the achievements hunters

If you would like to complete the game 100%, I recommend don’t be lazy and exploring every corner in the rooms to collect all the wooden figures.
It is possible to replay all the scenes in the chapters to get some specific achievements, so don’t worry if you couldn’t solve some puzzles – you can come back after finishing the game.

Overall

As for me, the game is slightly overpriced. I recommend waiting for a sale. The game in general was enjoyable but the amount of limitations which I spoke about do not allow me to recommend the game to everyone.
If you like narrative games and have a few evenings to play a game, you can give this game a chance.

2 people found this review funny 3

Not Recommended
8.4 hrs on record
Posted: 20 February, 2022
This game made me angry on so many levels. The story is so completely muddled and confused, unfocused on what its message or even its core theme is. It seems like the intended point is the sibling protagonists' search for truth as to why their mother tried to kill them when they were children. Now that desire for truth, an explanation, is completely believable. However, their doggedness in searching for it is downright harmful, because this is a situation where there simply are no answers, and the fact that they are presented with them in the form of a bloody storybook puzzle is aggravating. Normally I don't fault a story for not being realistic, but in this instance where so many deeply affecting topics are being handled, such flights of fancy are borderline offensive.

I myself lost a parent around the same as Tyler and Alison did, an abusive parent with deep mental health problems. It was so hard to balance what they'd done with my limited knowledge of them, how could this have happened, there was no reason, etc. There were times where I theorised someone had even poisoned them, or that maybe it was all a lie. But no matter how long I spent thinking and searching for answers, there were none, because the only person with those answers was gone. The reality is that when someone gets to a point where they're willing to do something unthinkable to themselves or their children, they are no longer able to open up to anyone, they are trapped inside themselves and noone can ever know why they do what they do, for there is no real, logical reason.

But this story insists for eight hours that it is not a fruitless search, and that Tyler's constant pressuring of his sister into continuing an actively hurtful pursuit of truth is actually the correct stance. That Alison wants to stop is actually selfish of her, that she "owes it" to him to hurt her mental health for non-existent answers. Tyler is incredibly pushy and demanding of everyone around him who's just trying to help, and though it's understandable why, the writing never pushes him to make remands or grow, and just fuels his vendetta.

Outside of this main grievance, I'll admit, it's a fine looking game, one of the best maybe. The snow deforms beneath your character's feet in a more believable fashion than I've ever seen, and this game achieves what Life is Strange 2 failed in terms of making a rural part of America truly breathtaking at points. The facial animation has some hiccups, but mostly holds up well, and the performances that go along with it are on the whole, pretty decent. Tyler and Alison both have a tendency to be flat in moments that should demand extreme emotion, but it does improve towards the end of the game.

All that being said, you'll notice the last word of that paragraph is "game", and, well, I'd hesitate to call this one. Compared to Life is Strange (which, to all intents and purposes, this is a LiS game published by Microsoft instead), there are fewer puzzles, fewer dialogue choices, fewer actual plot changing choices, fewer interactions with the game in general. You walk around a small environment for a few minutes, maybe examine some details to earn a trite observation, then enter into a twenty minute cutscene where you will make one solitary dialogue choice, then rinse and repeat over each three hour chapter.

The central power the twins have is also thoroughly underwhelming. It really doesn't add any actual mechanics or even much of interest to the story. It's also very ill-defined. They can visualise their respective memories in the world to each other, but sometimes combine their memory powers to see a true memory? Sometimes they can even remember things they can't possibly, such as faces that were obscured to them (which helps fuel a pointless twist that made me laugh out loud later in the game).

Overall, this game is just not worth it. Yes one of the main characters is trans, hooray, but all the cries of this being the new gold standard of queer games is outrageous, erasing years and years of trans indie creators who have been fighting for depictions of their story and more inclusive experiences in general. Not to mention that it overshadows this actually being a poorly handled story of grief over a parental abuser.

Arubek
0
26 people found this review helpful
2 people found this review funny 0

Not Recommended
2.6 hrs on record
Posted: 8 December, 2023
An interesting premise is soured by a lack of interactivity and player-input

From Mass Effect 2 to The Last of Us , LGBTQ+ characters have played a crucial role in some of gaming’s best narratives, and the rise of accessible game development software has enabled further diversity to grow within the sector – although socioeconomic and cultural barriers still remain. This accessibility, and the backing of mainstream precedents, has fostered the conditions to create brilliant LGBTQ+ stories with new games coming out every month.

Tell Me Why (and I know the song you’re now playing in your head) has been facilitated by the above conditions and casts a spotlight on a trans-centred narrative. The male presenting main character, Tyler, navigates themes of childhood trauma, religious discrimination, and queer persecution as he seeks to discover why those that raised him came to hold transphobic views.

The game explores anti-LGBTQ+ sentiment in a rural Alaskan town, with many residents holding strong traditional conservative values. While some characters are outright hostile, including religiously intolerant characters, Tell Me Why provides some nuance with some older characters feeling more confused rather than holding any particular malice towards LGBTQ+ people.

Tyler has more than enough reasons to be cynical towards his fellow Alaskans, but several characters get unfairly caught in the crossfire. Some absolutely deserved to be fought against, such as family-friend Tessa, but occasionally a well-intentioned yet naive character would be unfairly rung out on the same level as those outwardly transphobic. LGBTQ+ people are understandably exhausted by having to fight to justify their very existence, and so I understand this from a realism point of view, but narratively I felt the game encouraged me to negatively judge any awkward working class character that didn’t regularly interact with LGBTQ+ people, such as the sailor in the beginning of episode 1.

Many of these issues stem from a lack of control over Tyler. Tell Me Why is a story-focused game, meaning the majority of gametime is spent in conversation and examining interactive elements in the gameworld for lore and backstory. However, there’s a lack of any real decision making throughout. Conversations feel like they play out identically no matter what you pick, leading you to exhaust all dialogue options rather than take genuine care into your decisions. It was so strict that I’d have preferred the game be fully on the rails, with less gameplay elements, rather than faux-interactivity. I didn’t feel I could put my own influence onto Tyler’s personality, and so I was entirely playing out his own pre-determined story with no real choice in how he reacts to other characters.

For this reason, I lost interest quickly in Tell Me Why. I felt I was going through the motions, rather than meaningfully engaging in the narrative. Perhaps it gets better after 3 hours, but I didn’t feel any incentive to bash on after this. I look forward to playing more of DONTNOD’s games, but this one didn’t land for me.

venk
0
106 people found this review helpful
9 people found this review funny 11

Not Recommended
7.6 hrs on record
Posted: 21 October, 2020
Product received for free

Not even a heartache
From the developers of Life is Strange and Vampyr, Tell Me Why is an episodic adventure game where you control twins, Tyler and Alyson, as they revisit their old family home and relive the tragic events that lead to their separation. I'll be honest, I didn't like Life is Strange, and I gave Life is Strange: Before the Storm a negative review, so I was hoping that Dontnod would improve their future games and learn from their previous ones, especially in the animation department, but they didn’t with this one.

Pros
✔️ Interesting characters.
✔️ Beautiful graphics and a very detailed world.
✔️ Well-optimized, no technical issues.

Cons
❌ The story is very slow-paced, with cringe writing here and there.
❌ Needless supernatural abilities and boring fairy-tale that follows the main story.
❌ Almost all choices don't matter.
❌ Monotonous gameplay, with ridiculously easy puzzles.
❌ Awful lip-sync and facial animation.
❌ Forgettable soundtrack.

Story
The story takes place in a rural town in Alaska called Delos Crossing, and you switch perspectives between two characters, Alyson and Tyler Ronan. The twins are reunited for the first time in 10 years, after Tyler was sent away for the death of his mother, as they believe she tried to kill him for being transgender when he was young. They plan to sell their mother's home and move on to a new place, but they stumbled upon some remnants from the past that change their view of their mother and her motives, and it's up to them to find out what really happened on that dreadful day

Characters
Even though you play both characters, the story mainly focuses on Tyler; he is a trans man who wanted to transition when he was a kid, and thought his mother didn't like that idea and tried to kill him. As far as I know, this is the first playable transgender character in gaming. I initially thought that it would be the main focus of the game, but it subverted my expectations, and Dontnod handled it pretty good, so hats off to them.


The developers also included the National Suicide Prevention Lifeline number, and it shows up after you complete the game. 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: TFX


The side characters are interesting, such as Sam and Tessa; they are friendly and relatable, and most of them helped the twins during their mother's death. Their conversations and histories with the twins, and motivations, make them feel like real people you meet every day.

Fairy-tale & Supernatural abilities
Both Alyson and Tyler share an ability where they can telepathically communicate with one another. I guess to show how strong the bond is between them. They can also visualize memories in the real world, for some reason. Honestly, this felt unnecessary, and the same story could have been told without it (whispering instead of communicating telepathically and visualizing memories in their minds instead of in real-time). They could have told the story without all of this to make it feel more grounded, exciting and realistic.

They also managed to add a fairy-tale that ties with the story; the twins wrote the whole thing in a book with their mother before her death. The book is also used to solve most of the puzzles in the game. It felt dull and didn't really grab me. I guess they had to add it to lengthen the story, as it just drags on and tells it at a very slow pace. The game could've easily been 3 hours only, without the fairy-tale and supernatural elements.

Choices
Like in any interactive adventure games, the way to progress through the story is to choose certain actions and choices presented along the way. However, the majority of the choices were poor, in my opinion; most of them don't really matter, and the story doesn't adapt to them, there are no branching paths, they focus on tedious and unrelated things, and at the end of the day you either be a normal good person, or a pretentious dishonest douche who hates everyone (except for the final choice at least). I'll admit that there were a few choices that I liked, as they affect the bond level between the twins, but unfortunately, they don't really affect the ending.

Endings
The game has three chapters in total, with two distinct endings, which depend only on two choices in chapter 3. they are disappointing, since the bond level doesn't really affect the ending, and one of the final decisions didn't make sense to me ()

Other than that, the voice acting was alright, but the writing was a mixed bag and bland; some of the conversations between characters felt awkward at times, dialogue will make you squint your eyes and cringe, characters over-explain things that don't need to be, and the line delivery is sometimes off. The expressionless faces just add more salt to the wound.

Gameplay
The game mostly consists of cutscenes and fixed-camera conversations between characters. As for the gameplay portion, you can walk around, explore and interact with items and objects in the world. Although I admire the little details they added in the world, the problem is that it almost feels like a waste of time; when you interact with an object, either Tyler or Alyson will comment on it with a cringe joke, so I stopped interacting every object. There are a few puzzles in this game, some of which you have to use the fairy-tale book to get the right answer, but all of them are extremely easy and won't take much of your time. Overall, I think the gameplay parts of the Tell Me Why was not enjoyable.


The game is played in a third person perspective, with no HUD to make it more immersive. 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: TFX


Graphics
The game looks absolutely gorgeous; the world is so rich and detailed, you can leave footprints in the snow (and they stay there for a while), and the cloth physics is excellent. Dontnod nailed it in the graphics department. However, that doesn't apply to characters' facial expressions; some usually have expressionless faces, with only their mouths moving, and the lip sync is sometimes off. Dontnod's other games also suffered from this, and it explains why they didn't show characters talking on-screen in their trailers (same goes for their next game, Twin Mirror)


The stunning landscape of a small town in Alaska; snowy mountains, snow roofs, and a snowman of course. 
A Screenshot of Tell Me Why
By: TFX


Technical
Laptop: Omen HP 17t, GTX 1070, Intel I7-7700HQ, 16 GB RAM, Windows 10 Home
The game worked well and ran beautifully. No issues to report.

Soundtrack
Life is Strange and Life is Strange: Before the Storm had some great songs in their soundtracks, but that's not the case for this game; they are forgettable, bland, basic and none of the songs stood out to me (one song, Stop The Stars by Tiny Deaths, was fine I guess, but not great). I don't know what emotion they tried to convey with their music; they are just that bland.

Additional Details:
Total playtime	7.5 hours
M&K or Controller	Both work fine
Offline mode	Yes
Trading cards	None
Games Saves	Automatically

Final Thoughts
One step forward, two steps back, that's how I would describe this game. Life is Strange, with all its faults, is a much better game than this; the writing is uninspiring, choices don't matter, the ending is disappointing, the gameplay is lacking and the soundtrack is forgettable. Definitely not worth the asking price.

for more quality reviews like this, follow our curator page, Devils in the Detail.

Initially this game started very interesting with a murder plot and 2 traumatized siblings but sadly missing real choices and a kind of a slog mechanic they brought us with the "story book" which you always have to finish even if you wanna rather continue the real story makes it not as good as Life is Strange.

5.0 hrs on record
Posted: 11 March, 2021
This game is terrible; I think the first life is strange was an accident because everything don't nod made after in a similar format failed to capture what made it such a good game.
Firstly, the most glaring issue is pacing; the first game always managed to get straight to the point, Chloe gets shot within the first 30 mins of the game Max receives her powers. Before the first episode even ends, you have the Rachel amber conspiracy, Max's powers, Nathans's drug problem already set up. You were never stuck in an area doing nothing, you could look around and read things, but the plot was always moving when you were ready.
Your power is cut scenes in "tell me why"; you spend the first 4 hours wandering around a house.
The most egregious is the convenience store where you have to walk around looking for cut scenes; it's tedious and annoying.
The second problem is this dual protagonist; the Max/Chloe dynamic worked because you were Max first and foremost, so you had a degree of separation from Chloe. You also suffered solely for your choices.
In this game, you alternate between shooting yourself in the foot and having a chip on your shoulder about it. You can make a choice as Tyler, then switch to Alyson, and suddenly for "plot," Tyler negates your prior intention and causes a conflict.
It means you aren't invested in either because there are four main characters; the Tyler and Alyson you play, and the Tyler and Alyson in the cut-scenes.

Tl;DR don't bother with this game or this studio; they don't know what they're doing.

One of the biggest issues with "Tell Me Why" is the pacing. The game feels slow and dragged out, with long stretches of dialogue and exploration that fail to keep the player engaged. While the story is intriguing, the game takes too long to deliver its narrative, resulting in a lack of tension and excitement.

Additionally, the game's mechanics are clunky and unrefined. The puzzles are simplistic and repetitive, and the gameplay feels like an afterthought, with little to no challenge or depth. The character animations and voice acting also leave much to be desired, with stiff movements and flat delivery that fails to bring the characters to life.

Another issue with "Tell Me Why" is its lack of player agency. The game's narrative is heavily scripted, with very little room for player choice or consequence. While the illusion of choice is present, the game ultimately funnels the player towards a predetermined outcome, resulting in a lack of replayability.

Don't play this crap

11.7 hrs on record
Posted: 19 December, 2021
Tell Me Why in part has a powerful story to tell and has the stage to bring insight to those inside and outside the subject matter.
It's a shame this is coupled with a game that fails it's predecessors.
Tell Me Why has failed to deliver an experience comparable to Life is Strange.
This game is heavily story driven.
The Twins stories are stimulating but let down by the execution.
At first (CH1) you are able to explore and interact with objects, allowing you to delve into a characters past and giving you an deeper insight into the character in question or the story at hand.
However I felt that in the subsequent two chapters the amount of object interaction for the purpose of deepening the story reduces.
Instead the bulk of the "story telling" comes in the form of interactions with NPC's and the Twins special connection.
Whilst these two methods in theory are fine to portray a story and it's intricate details, my experience with TMW diminished their effectiveness. This was due to the placement of my "choice of reply" text on-screen as (in the case of longer replies) some of the text was missing (off the edge of the screen), and with the added time constraint to answer I felt unable to consider my answer before choosing.
With the way the save function works replaying a particular scene just to hear a single conversation again can mean you have to go through prior conversations or interactions before hand.
The "Memories" that also portray the story appear to have a flaw in that I seem to have been able to, in some scenes access those memories in no particular order complicating my understanding of the whole picture because the info i'm receiving is jumbled up.
Tell Me Why has puzzles to solve throughout the game and I really enjoyed the puzzles in LiS, however the puzzles in TMW whilst satisfying to solve are lacking the interactive complexity found in LiS.
For example: in TMW you mostly interact with a single object and perform some action or actions, with the exception of the "archive room". In LiS some puzzles involved interaction with multiple objects in order to preform a final single action to achieve an end goal.
I Loved the story and wished there was more, by this I mean more content, my game time difference between LiS and TMW is large.
In addition to content, each chapter from 1 through to 3 seemed to have less interaction from me and more cut-scenes to watch respectively.
Included and portrayed in the story are a few "Real Life" social groups, however I felt a slight bias from one to another by portraying each social group with the traits of the character connected to that social group. Bias has no place in any media, and the slight changes to dialogue and scenes in order to remove this sense of bias would not detract from the main story at all.
With the addition that TMW does not support full 21:9 aspect ratio in the current market further disappointment is experienced.
To sum up, I can't recommend that you purchase TMW.

 

Garp02
97 products in account
0
4 people found this review helpful 0

Not Recommended
9.8 hrs on record
Posted: 16 June, 2021
Product received for free

NOTE: This game was obtained for free as part of a Pride Month promotion in June 2021.

Tell Me Why is a slow-paced adventure game developed by DONONOT, the creators of Life is Strange 1. The game’s gameplay is very similar to LiS1’s, but features some new twists and a completely different story. How does it actually stack up, though?

Gameplay is a fairly typical modern point and click game. Your character is in a certain location, and in order to proceed, he or she must explore the area to learn more about the area, discover a problem or create a plan, and find a way to solve the problem or execute the plan. It’s simple, but fun.

You also have conversations with other people, where you can pick responses to what characters say. The controls for this can be a bit awkward, as you have to press a button on initiate a conversation instead of the response prompt appearing automatically, but it does allow for things like responding to someone while you explore instead of accidentally canceling a response prompt because it appeared just as you began examining something.

The choices affect you make affect small details of how the story works. For example, being nice to characters will make them more willing to open up, while being a jerk will not. However, no matter how you play, it will have very little impact on the ending, which is determined solely by a choice you make at the very end and a choice near the middle of the third chapter. It’s disappointing to see that the developers didn’t really learn from Life is Strange and made most of your choices irrelevant at the end again.

There is also an option that lets you psychically communicate with your twin during conversations in order to unlock alternate dialogue trees and ways to solve problems, such as calming down a situation that could lead to the person you’re talking to becoming belligerent. Unfortunately, this cool feature is underused, making it feel more like a gimmick than a proper part of the gameplay.
The twins’ psychic abilities can also be used to conjure up memories in order to advance the plot. These are not only used to create in-game flashbacks to events (which is important, since this game is all about discovering what actually happened in the past with the twins and their mother), they’re also used choose how certain events played out. While fun, your choices don’t really lead to much.

Finally, there’s a sort of “karma” system that tracks your responses on certain things. In theory, the idea is that certain choices that are nice to the twin you’re not playing as will improve their bonds, while ones that are antagonistic to the other twin will weaken the bond. Part of the ending changes depending on how strong the bond is at the end of the game. While a neat idea in theory, most of the “correct” choices are about sucking up to Tyler and saying he’s right, even in situations where he might be wrong or the answer is not clear. Only a few “correct” answers are about how Alyson feels, so the mechanic feels more like “how much can you kiss Tyler’s butt” like you’re trying to befriend a companion in a Bioware game than actually building a strong bond between the twins.

The game’s story focuses on a set of twins. One of them killed his mother when she attempted to murder him as a child ten years ago, and is now leaving juvie in order to help his sister, Alyson, sell the family’s home. However, the events of 10 years ago are not what they seem and the people in the small Alaskan town the twins live in are unwilling to discuss what happened that day and the months leading up to it.

The game is as it’s peak when it has the characters directly involved in the main plotline, investigating what really happened the night the twins’ mother was killed. Looking through old memories and documents to piece together what happened and using what you’ve learned to get the truth out of people unwilling to spit it out is very fun. Chapter 2 is filled with the nitty-gritty of the investigating, making it the most fun chapter out of the three.

However, the game does not spend all of it’s time on the main plotline. In fact, the main plotline does not truly start until the player is deep in the first chapter! Instead, it spends a lot of time focusing on more personal moments. This would be fine if the moments were fairly short, but they end up eating too much time in the story. Having moments to cool down between investigations is fine, but these parts seem to drag on forever with nonsense like “slowly walk around the twins’ house while labeling things and listening to the twins make witty comments with each other”, “calmly explore a general store”, and “yet another dull romantic sequence with Tyler’s love interest”. These makes the game feel like it’s more interested in spinning its wheels than moving things forward. Life is Strange 1 managed to hit a good balance between investigations and more personal moments, so it’s disappointing to see Tell Me Why completely fumble it.

Early on in the story, the twins (re)discover an old book of homemade fairy tales created by them and their mother called “The Book of Goblins”. What should’ve been a cute background element instead plays a vital role in both the gameplay and the plot. At some points, your characters will have to solve a puzzle, and conveniently, the solution is found in one of the stories in The Book of Goblins. The solutions are not just in the story, but also in the art included in the stories. These slow down the game significantly as you crawl though the story the game tells you to look through to find a solution. It feels like a hackened way to tie the gameplay into the overall story that just drags the game down as a whole.

The Book of Goblins leads to another issue with the story; there are a ridiculous amount of mechanical puzzles in a cabin located far away from civilization. It’s understandable that the developers need to add puzzles to the game so there’s more to do besides examine objects and talk to people, but it’s pretty ridiculous that a two-story cabin seems to have as many obtuse puzzles and mechanical devices as Resident Evil’s Spenser Mansion or Raccoon City’s Police Department HQ. Did the twins’ mom work for Umbrella Corporation or something?

The biggest issue with the story is how much it makes Tyler the center of the world without giving some sort of good reason why. Based on how the story acts, you’d think that Tyler is some chosen one that everyone needs to ass-kiss, but he’s just some nobody that never earns the characters lining up to stroke his ego. To his credit, he is the main agent that keeps the plot moving forward, but the amount of focus and love he gets becomes grating fairly quick.

Tyler is special enough that he gets his own romantic interest, Michael. Michael is probably the most pointless character in the series. The scenes he’s in drag the story and pacing to a crawl and contribute almost nothing to the overall story. His voice acting is about a step or two about porn acting, and he dresses like a tremendous tool.

Tell me Why starts off with an interesting premise, but seems to be unable to stick to what makes it work best. Between underutilizing an interesting mechanic, too much focus on one of the twins, and uneven pacing, the story and gameplay is just too uneven to really recommend.

10.7 hrs on record
Posted: 20 March, 2021
The game doesn't tell you why.

On a more serious note, I am very disappointed with this game.
I loved Dontnod's previous work Life is Strange and while I wasn't expecting this game to be even close to being as good as LiS, I was still expecting it to be somewhat good.
This is mediocre at best, poor storytelling. Lots of wasting time around and useless intrigue that lead to nothing in the end. This could've easily been a 3 hours movie, instead it's a 10 hours game.

The "gameplay" sections felt more like a chore to play through instead of adding something interesting to the story. Unlike Life is Strange where the gameplay added to the immersion & gave you interesting choices that somewhat impact the story, this game does none of that.
Gameplay feels like an afterthought and none of the "choices" you make really matter. The story will play out exactly the same for everyone and none of it impacts anything around you.
There are some puzzles to solve in this game and while the game does give you the option to skip all of them, I still want to point out that they are incredibly boring to solve. I bought a game, not a book.

So, how's the story? Well, if I'm being honest it has good moments for sure. I liked parts of it and some scenes are definitely worth viewing but they are so few and far between that ultimately I don't recommend you play through all the useless crap to get to them.
There are some characters that I liked and most of them have interesting dialogue but some of them I couldn't bring myself to care for. The playable characters are fine but I found it hard to connect with them as they would always talk about memories we, the player, don't know about and act as if we know what happened. It just feels awkward to hear them talk about these stories we know nothing about as if we were there. I found it frustrating and also, the intrigue drags on so much to the point where I was screaming at my screen "JUST TELL ME WHY ALREADY!".

Graphics are beautiful. That's about the only thing I can 100% say is good about this game, it looks gorgeous. Environments felt lived in & were always a pleasure to look at. Atmosphere for the most part is done very well as you'd come to expect from this type of game. Music was pretty good too, came in at the right moments and was never overused or unnecessary.

Conclusion, episode 1 is now free to play so i'd recommend you play it for half an hour and decide if you care enough to keep going. 50% of the players don't have the episode 1 completed achievement so I'll let that speak for itself.
 

lollipop

13.8 hrs on record
Posted: 12 September, 2022
Just a quick info: If you want to play this game, try to get it for free or something. There have been multiple times already that "Tell Me Why" has been one of those games you could get for free on several different stores - especially when it's "Pride Month" as there's not too many games that much on the nose with the whole topic - plus it probably isn't too much of a loss, either to do that. Now onto the real deal:

Tell Me Why is about the twins Alyson and Tyler Ronan. They both grew up in a pretty small town in kind of a make-shift house with their mother Mary-Ann. Right at the beginning of the game you get a glimpse into their childhood. Tyler as a kid (and before transitioning) is telling a cop about how he stabbed his own mother, because she tried to kill him for cutting his hair - presumably because she couldn't deal with his trans-identity. Fast forward to the present where you see how Tyler is now getting out of a youth facility for troubled kids and Alyson, his sister, is picking him up in order to drive up to their old mother's house that they intend to put on sale in order to get rid of the unpleasant memories of the past and actually get some cash to get their lives started. While Tyler was more or less locked away in the youth facility, Alyson got adopted by the cop who interrogated Tyler that night - police chief Eddy Brown. Alyson's and Tyler's relationship is somewhat strained after all these years and you as the player now get to experience what will become of it. Of course, you'll also try to find out about the twins' past with their deceased mother.

Well, the whole premise of the game sounds interesting and I truly enjoyed the views. But that's probably about it. The gameplay is pretty much standard for Dontnod games. You get to choose between two options and then see if the game punishes or rewards you for it. I actually did like the game for the first half maybe? That was mostly because of the mysteries surrounding the twins' mother though. But after a certain time, I felt like the payout could never be as great as the whole buildup to it and at the end I did feel as if my guess was pretty much right on the money here. A lot of the things people hide from you seem pretty stupid when it comes to how traumatized Tyler and Alyson were as kids from assuming certain things that simply weren't true. While sure, Tyler was practically locked away, I don't really understand why Alyson never had a heart to heart with the people she saw every day and who could have talked with her about her mother and the night she died. The gap between that past event and "now" as in the time period you are playing this game in, feels too large to make sense when it comes to all the stuff you find out in the game. Also the whole thing about Tyler being trans AND struggling with it even more due to his mother and the circumstances of her death made so many story elements and character decisions over the years seem even more nonsensical to me. Like I wanted to take those people and shake them real hard, screaming into their faces: "Talk you ♥♥♥♥♥♥♥ piece of ♥♥♥♥! Ever heard about communication?!" Especially because it didn't take thaaaat long to get it out of them now. So what? Are a dozen years of trauma and misunderstandings a joke to you people?

All in all, you get to meet all these characters who are very secretive about what happened in the past and it's nice to get to know them and try to figure all that stuff out, but meh. It's nothing groundbreaking. You don't get enough time with the characters to actually come to like them or even care about them and the romance option they shove into the game felt pretty nonsensical to me - especially how you only get to say: Yes or No instead of well going like: "Sorry, but this is a highly emotional time for me now so can we hold off on any talk about romance - especially because I've only known you for like 2 days top?!" It felt a bit...off all things considered.

And even though I mostly liked the twins as characters and their bond, I must admit that most choices felt kind of nonsensical to me, as I was always limited to do one thing in order to not upset one of the siblings. There was usually no other way than upsetting the person in front of you enough to fight or simply agreeing with them to keep the peace. What I like in choice-based games is when they aren't so clear-cut. You struggle with a choice, because all options feel valid to you, not because you want to see the good or bad ending - at least in my humble opinion. It's also nice to not automatically know what answer leads to which outcome; of course, there's also a limit as to how much unpredictability you want here, but if the outcomes logically make sense and maybe the game isn't too long so that you can easily try out a different option later, I think it's great to show more nuances to your decisions, because they in turn show that your characters have more depth than you might think.

Plus: Most of the supernatural looking stuff just fell away by being depicted as some kind of coping mechanism the twins used to deal with their surroundings in the past even though it is clearly just a way to hide certain characters from you for the sake of the flimsy story in the game in order to stretch everything out. Like: Sure, after so many years things get blurry, but remembering a key person they knew and interacted with on a nearly daily basis only as some kind of bad fairytale character their mom had told them about seemed too far of a stretch.
Oh, and this game loves its plot twists which rather than exciting me made me feel bored out of my mind after some time. “Ooooh, this thing wasn’t that thing – AGAIN? I totally didn’t see that one coming…” After the first half, I only wanted it to be over to get this game off my list.

The story didn't really land with me. And that’s a bad thing in a game where story is everything and there's no real gameplay mechanic to turn it around.

Well, that's just what I think though. I know there are people liking this game and enjoying it and all the power to you! I did, too - in parts. I just didn't feel like this game was as good as it's made out to be. After some time I just ceased to care about what happens to the characters or the plot in general. But at least I can say that this game doesn't have an annoying Chloe in it. Scratch all I said before - 10/10, I do not have to deal with an obnoxious, annoying Chloe Price. What do I want more out of a game by Dontnod?
